Stuttgart's hilly cityscape from atop the <span lang="de"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Karlsh%C3%B6he_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Karlshöhe (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Karlshöhe</a></span></div>

<p><b>Stuttgart</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'oo' in 'foot'">ʊ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><small title="English pronunciation respelling"><i><b>SHTUUT</b>-gart</i></small></a>;<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <small>German:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈʃtʊtɡaʁt]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Stuttgart.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Stuttgart.ogg" class="internal" title="Stuttgart.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; <a href="/wiki/Swabian_German" title="Swabian German">Swabian</a>: <i><span lang="swg">Schduagert</span></i>, <small>pronounced</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Alemannic_German" title="Help:IPA/Alemannic German">[ˈʒ̊d̥ua̯ɡ̊ɛʕd̥]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Names_of_European_cities_in_different_languages:_Q-T#S" class="mw-redirect" title="Names of European cities in different languages: Q-T">names in other languages</a>) is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities in Germany">largest city</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/German_states" class="mw-redirect" title="German states">state</a> of <span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a></span>.</p>
<p>Stuttgart is located on the <a href="/wiki/Neckar" title="Neckar">Neckar</a> river in a fertile valley locally known as the "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart_Cauldron&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stuttgart Cauldron (page does not exist)">Stuttgart Cauldron</a>" an hour from the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_Jura" title="Swabian Jura">Swabian Jura</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a>, and its urban area has a population of 609,219,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Germany_by_population" title="List of cities in Germany by population">sixth largest city</a> in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> 2.7 million people live in the city's administrative region<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and another 5.3 million people in its <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Metropolitan_Region" title="Stuttgart Metropolitan Region">metropolitan area</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> making it the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_regions_in_Germany" title="Metropolitan regions in Germany">fourth largest metropolitan area in Germany</a>.</p>
<p>The city and metropolitan area are consistently ranked among the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_in_the_European_Union_by_GDP" title="List of metropolitan areas in the European Union by GDP">top 20 European metropolitan areas by GDP</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mercer_(consulting_firm)" title="Mercer (consulting firm)">Mercer</a> listed Stuttgart as 21st on its 2015 <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_quality_of_living" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities by quality of living">list of cities by quality of living</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[a]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mercer2015_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mercer2015-9">[9]</a></sup> innovation agency 2thinknow ranked the city 24th globally out of 442 cities <sup id="cite_ref-2think2015_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2think2015-11">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[b]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Globalization and World Cities Research Network</a> ranked the city as a Beta-status <a href="/wiki/World_city" class="mw-redirect" title="World city">world city</a> in their 2014 survey.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 6th millennium BC, the Stuttgart area has been an important agricultural area and has been host to a number of cultures seeking to utilize the rich soil of the <a href="/wiki/Neckar_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Neckar valley">Neckar valley</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> conquered the area in 83 AD and built a massive <span lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Castrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Castrum">castrum</a></span> near <a href="/wiki/Bad_Cannstatt" title="Bad Cannstatt">Bad Cannstatt</a>, making it the most important regional center for several centuries. Stuttgart's roots were truly laid in the 10th century with its founding by <a href="/wiki/Liudolf,_Duke_of_Swabia" title="Liudolf, Duke of Swabia">Liudolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Swabia" title="Duke of Swabia">Duke of Swabia</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Stud_farm" title="Stud farm">stud farm</a> for his warhorses. Overshadowed by nearby <a href="/wiki/Cannstatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Cannstatt">Cannstatt</a>, the town grew steadily and was granted a charter in 1320. The fortunes of Stuttgart turned with those of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="House of Württemberg">House of Württemberg</a>, and they made it the capital of their <a href="/wiki/County_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="County of Württemberg">county</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Duchy of Württemberg">duchy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Kingdom of Württemberg">kingdom</a> from the 15th century to 1918. Stuttgart prospered despite setbacks in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> and devastating air raids by the Allies on the city and its automobile production. However, by 1952, the city had bounced back and it became the major economic, industrial, tourism and publishing center it is today.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stuttgart is also a transport junction, and possesses the sixth-largest airport in Germany, where companies such as <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a> ,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dinkelacker" title="Dinkelacker">Dinkelacker</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> and others are headquartered there.</p>
<p>Stuttgart is unusual in the scheme of German cities.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> It is spread across a variety of hills (some of them covered in <a href="/wiki/Vineyard" title="Vineyard">vineyards</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Valleys" class="mw-redirect" title="Valleys">valleys</a> (especially around the <a href="/wiki/Neckar_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Neckar river">Neckar river</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart_basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stuttgart basin (page does not exist)">Stuttgart basin</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart#Parks" title="Stuttgart">parks</a>. This is often a source of surprise to visitors who associate the city with its reputation as the "<a href="/wiki/Stuttgart#The_cradle_of_the_automobile" title="Stuttgart">cradle of the automobile</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Amondson_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amondson-22">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> The city's tourism <a href="/wiki/Slogan" title="Slogan">slogan</a> is "Stuttgart offers more".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> Under current plans to improve transport links to the international infrastructure (as part of the <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_21" title="Stuttgart 21">Stuttgart 21</a> project), the city unveiled a new logo and slogan in March 2008 describing itself as "<i><span lang="de">Das neue Herz Europas</span></i>" ("The new Heart of Europe").<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> For business, it describes itself as "Where business meets the future". In July 2010, Stuttgart unveiled a new city logo, designed to entice more business people to stay in the city and enjoy breaks in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stuttgart is a city with a high number of immigrants. According to <a href="/wiki/Dorling_Kindersley" title="Dorling Kindersley">Dorling Kindersley</a>'s <i><a href="#CITEREFDorling2001">Eyewitness Travel Guide to Germany</a></i>, "In the city of Stuttgart, every third inhabitant is a foreigner."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorling200115_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorling200115-27">[25]</a></sup> 40% of Stuttgart's residents, and 64% of the population below the age of five, are of immigrant background.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Stuttgart, often nicknamed the "<i>Schwabenmetropole</i>" (English: <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabian</a> metropolis) in reference to its location in the center of <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_German" title="Swabian German">local dialect</a> spoken by the native <a href="/wiki/Swabians" title="Swabians">Swabians</a>, has its etymological roots in the <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> word <i>Stuotgarten</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt1-29">[27]</a></sup> or "<a href="/wiki/Stud_farm" title="Stud farm">stud farm</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-StuttInfo_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuttInfo-30">[28]</a></sup> because the city was founded in 950&#160;<a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a> by <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">Duke</a> <a href="/wiki/Liudolf,_Duke_of_Swabia" title="Liudolf, Duke of Swabia">Liudolf</a> <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Swabia" title="Duke of Swabia">of Swabia</a> to breed warhorses.<sup id="cite_ref-EBStutt_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBStutt-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Stuttgart" title="History of Stuttgart">History of Stuttgart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Stuttgart" title="Timeline of Stuttgart">Timeline of Stuttgart</a></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stuttgart_Rathaus_Marktplatz_1907.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stuttgart Rathaus on the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Marktplatz_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Marktplatz (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Marktplatz</a>, <a href="/wiki/1907_in_Germany" title="1907 in Germany">1907</a>. The building was destroyed by <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_Two" class="mw-redirect" title="Allies of World War Two">Allied</a> <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Stuttgart_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Stuttgart in World War II">bombing</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_Two" class="mw-redirect" title="World War Two">World War Two</a>. What was left of the building was used to build the current City Hall.</div>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Originally, the most important location in the <a href="/wiki/Neckar" title="Neckar">Neckar</a> river valley as the hilly rim of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart_basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stuttgart basin (page does not exist)">Stuttgart basin</a> at what is today <a href="/wiki/Bad_Cannstatt" title="Bad Cannstatt">Bad Cannstatt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> Thus, the first settlement of Stuttgart was a massive <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Castra" title="Castra">Castra</a> stativa</i> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Cannstatt_Castrum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cannstatt Castrum (page does not exist)">Cannstatt Castrum</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-EBStutt_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBStutt-31">[29]</a></sup> built c. <a href="/w/index.php?title=90_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="90 in Germany (page does not exist)">90</a> <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a> to protect the Villas and vineyards blanketing the landscape and the road from <a href="/wiki/Mogontiacum" class="mw-redirect" title="Mogontiacum">Mogontiacum</a> (Mainz) to <a href="/wiki/Augusta_Vindelicorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Augusta Vindelicorum">Augusta Vindelicorum</a> (Augsburg). As with many military installations, a settlement sprang up nearby and remained there even after the <a href="/wiki/Limes" title="Limes">Limes</a> moved further east. When they did, the town was left in the capable hands of a local <a href="/wiki/Brickwork" title="Brickwork">brickworks</a> that produced sophisticated architectural ceramics and pottery.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> When the Romans were driven back past the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> rivers in the <a href="/wiki/3rd_Century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="3rd Century in Germany">3rd century</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEG.C3.BChring200452_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEG.C3.BChring200452-35">[32]</a></sup> the settlement temporarily vanished from history until the <a href="/wiki/7th_Century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="7th Century in Germany">7th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-36">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Germany_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Germany in the Middle Ages">Germany in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/w/index.php?title=700_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="700 in Germany (page does not exist)">700</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Alemannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Alemannia">Duke</a> <a href="/wiki/Gotfrid" title="Gotfrid">Gotfrid</a> mentions a "Chan Stada" in a document regarding property.<sup id="cite_ref-Pangloss_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pangloss-37">[34]</a></sup> Archaeological evidence shows that later <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingian</a> era <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> farmers continued to till the same land the Romans did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirn2007_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirn2007-38">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cannstatt is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint_Gall" title="Abbey of Saint Gall">Abbey of St. Gall</a>'s archives as "<i>Canstat ad Neccarum</i>" (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de">Cannstatt-on-Neckar</span>) in <a href="/w/index.php?title=708_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="708 in Germany (page does not exist)">708</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> of the name "<i>Cannstatt</i>" is not clear, but as the site is mentioned as <i>condistat</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Annals_of_Metz" class="mw-redirect" title="Annals of Metz">Annals of Metz</a> (<a href="/wiki/9th_Century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="9th Century in Germany">9th century</a>),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> it is mostly derived from the Latin word <i>condita</i> ("foundation"), suggesting that the name of the Roman settlement might have had the prefix "<i>Condi-</i>." Alternatively, Sommer (1992) suggested that the Roman site corresponds to the <i>Civitas Aurelia G</i> attested to in an inscription found near <a href="/wiki/%C3%96hringen" title="Öhringen">Öhringen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> There have also been attempts at a derivation from a Gaulish <i>*kondâti-</i> "confluence".<sup id="cite_ref-auto_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-36">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/w/index.php?title=950_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="950 in Germany (page does not exist)">950</a> <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liudolf,_Duke_of_Swabia" title="Liudolf, Duke of Swabia">Duke Liudolf</a> of <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a>, son of the current <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto I</a>, decided to establish a stud farm for his cavalry during the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_invasions_of_Europe" title="Hungarian invasions of Europe">Hungarian invasions of Europe</a> on a widened area of the <a href="/wiki/Nesenbach" title="Nesenbach">Nesenbach</a> river valley 5 kilometres (3.1&#160;mi) south of the old Roman castrum.<sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> The land and title of <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Swabia" title="Duke of Swabia">Duke of Swabia</a> remained in Liudolf's hands until his rebellion was quashed by his father four years later. In 1089, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bruno_of_Calw&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bruno of Calw (page does not exist)">Bruno of Calw</a> built the precursor building to the <a href="/wiki/Old_Castle_(Stuttgart)" title="Old Castle (Stuttgart)">Old Castle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pangloss_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pangloss-37">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stuttgart's viticulture, first documented in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> in the year 1108 AD,<sup id="cite_ref-Pangloss_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pangloss-37">[34]</a></sup> kept people in the area of that stud farm for some time, but the area was still largely overshadowed by nearby Cannstatt because of its role as a local crossroad for many major European trade routes.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldTravGuide_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldTravGuide-41">[38]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the existence of a settlement here (despite the terrain being more suited for that original stud farm) during the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> is provided by a gift registry from <a href="/wiki/Hirsau_Abbey" title="Hirsau Abbey">Hirsau Abbey</a> dated to around 1160 that mentions a "Hugo de Stuokarten."<sup id="cite_ref-Pangloss_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pangloss-37">[34]</a></sup> A settlement at this locale was again mentioned in 1229, but this time by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Pope Gregory IX</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt1_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt1-29">[27]</a></sup> In 1219 AD, Stuttgart (then Stuotgarten) became a possession of <a href="/wiki/Herman_V,_Margrave_of_Baden-Baden" title="Herman V, Margrave of Baden-Baden">Herman V</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Baden" title="List of rulers of Baden">Margrave of Baden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt1_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt1-29">[27]</a></sup> In addition to <a href="/wiki/Backnang" title="Backnang">Backnang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pforzheim" title="Pforzheim">Pforzheim</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Besigheim" title="Besigheim">Besigheim</a>, Hermann would also found the Stuttgart we know today in c. 1220.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> In 1251, the city passed to the <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_I,_Count_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Ulrich I, Count of Württemberg">Ulrich I</a> <a href="/wiki/House_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="House of Württemberg">von Württemberg</a> as part of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mechthild_von_Baden&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mechthild von Baden (page does not exist)">Mechthild von Baden</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>. His son, <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_I,_Count_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Eberhard I, Count of Württemberg">Eberhard I</a> "the Illustrious,"<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt1_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt1-29">[27]</a></sup> would be the first to begin the many major expansions of Stuttgart under the House of Württemberg.</p>
<p>Eberhard desired to expand the realm his father had built through military action with the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Anti-king" title="Anti-king">anti-king</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Raspe,_Landgrave_of_Thuringia" title="Henry Raspe, Landgrave of Thuringia">Henry Raspe IV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landgrave_of_Thuringia" class="mw-redirect" title="Landgrave of Thuringia">Landgrave of Thuringia</a>, but was thwarted by the action of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_I_of_Germany" title="Rudolf I of Germany">Rudolph I</a>. Further resistance by Eberhard I against the Emperor's created <a href="/wiki/Vogt" title="Vogt">Vogts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick" title="Bailiwick">Bailiwicks</a> as well as the newly appointed Duke of Swabia <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_II,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Rudolf II, Duke of Austria">Rudolf II, Duke of Austria</a> eventually led to armed conflict and initial successes upon Emperor Rudolph I's death in 1291 against the Emperor's men. After initially defeating his regional rivals, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VII, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VII</a>, newly elected as Emperor, decided to take action against Eberhard I in 1311 during his <a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperial_War_of_1311&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Imperial War of 1311 (page does not exist)">war with the</a> <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">Free imperial city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Esslingen_am_Neckar" title="Esslingen am Neckar">Esslingen</a> by ordering his Vogt, Konrad IV von Weinberg, to declare war on Eberhard I. Eberhard I, defeated on the battlefield, lost Stuttgart and his castle (razed in 1311)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorling2001294_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorling2001294-43">[40]</a></sup> to Esslingen and the city was thus managed by the city state from 1312 to 1315.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt1_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt1-29">[27]</a></sup> Total destruction of the County was prevented by Henry VII's death in 24 August 1313 and the elections of <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Louis IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Louis IV</a> as King of the Germans and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Fair" title="Frederick the Fair">Frederick III</a> as anti-king. Eberhard seized the opportunity granted to him by the political chaos, and recaptured his hometown and birthplace in 1316,<sup id="cite_ref-GesichichtePDF_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GesichichtePDF-44">[41]</a></sup> and made much territorial gain. With peace restored at last, Eberhard began repairs and expansion to Stuttgart beginning with the reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Wirtemberg_Castle" title="Wirtemberg Castle">Wirtemberg Castle</a>, ancestral home to the House of Württemberg, in 1317 and then began expansion of the city's defenses. The early 1320s were an important one for Stuttgart: Eberhard I moved the seat of the County to the city to a <a href="/wiki/Old_Castle_(Stuttgart)" title="Old Castle (Stuttgart)">new and expanded castle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-LanMus:AltSch_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LanMus:AltSch-45">[42]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_church" title="Collegiate church">collegiate church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beutelsbach_(Weinstadt)" title="Beutelsbach (Weinstadt)">Beutelsbach</a>, where previous members of the Württemberg dynasty had been buried prior to its destruction in 1311,<sup id="cite_ref-GesichichtePDF_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GesichichtePDF-44">[41]</a></sup> moved to its <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg_Mausoleum" title="Württemberg Mausoleum">current location in Stuttgart</a> in 1320,<sup id="cite_ref-GesichichtePDF_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GesichichtePDF-44">[41]</a></sup> and the town's Stiftkirche was expanded into an abbey, and the control of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_Saint_Martin_(Plieningen)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Church of Saint Martin (Plieningen) (page does not exist)">Martinskirche</a> by the Bishopric of Constance was broken by Papal order in 1321.<sup id="cite_ref-GesichichtePDF_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GesichichtePDF-44">[41]</a></sup> A year after the city became the principal seat of the Counts of Württemberg in 1320,<sup id="cite_ref-EBStutt_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBStutt-31">[29]</a></sup> the city was granted status as a city and given civic rights.<sup id="cite_ref-EBStutt_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBStutt-31">[29]</a></sup> At the end of the <a href="/wiki/14th_century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="14th century in Germany">14th Century</a>, new suburbs sprang up around <a href="/w/index.php?title=Leonnhard_Church_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Leonnhard Church (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Leonhard Church</a> and near the city's fortifications as well. Towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/15th_century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="15th century in Germany">15th Century</a>, Count <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_V,_Count_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Ulrich V, Count of Württemberg">Ulrich V</a> began construction of a new suburb on the northeastern edge of the city around the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hospital_Church_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hospital Church (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Hospitalkirche</a>. In the 1457, the first Landtag of the <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Estates of Württemberg">Estates of Württemberg</a> was established in Stuttgart and a similar institution was established in <a href="/wiki/Leonberg" title="Leonberg">Leonberg</a>. After the temporary partitions of the County of Württemberg by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_N%C3%BCrtingen" title="Treaty of Nürtingen">Treaties of Nürtingen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_M%C3%BCnsingen" title="Treaty of Münsingen">Münsingen</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Treaty_of_Esslingen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Treaty of Esslingen (page does not exist)">Esslingen</a>, Stuttgart was once again declared the capital of the County in 1483.<sup id="cite_ref-GesichichtePDF_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GesichichtePDF-44">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_Era">Early Modern Era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Modern Era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1488, Stuttgart finally <i>officially</i> became the de facto residence of the Count himself as opposed to the location of his home, the Old Castle.<sup id="cite_ref-StuttInfo_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuttInfo-30">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_I,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Eberhard I, Duke of Württemberg">Eberhard I</a>, then Count Eberhard V, became the first <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Württemberg">Duke of Württemberg</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[d]</a></sup> in <a href="/w/index.php?title=1495_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1495 Germany (page does not exist)">1495</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> and made Stuttgart the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Duchy of Württemberg">Duchy of Württemberg</a> in addition to the County thereof. All this would be lost to the Württembergs during the reign of his son, <a href="/wiki/Ulrich,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Ulrich, Duke of Württemberg">Ulrich</a>. Though Ulrich initially made territorial gains as a result of his decision to fight alongside the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-EBUlrichDuke_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBUlrichDuke-47">[43]</a></sup> he was no friend of the powerful <a href="/wiki/Swabian_League" title="Swabian League">Swabian League</a> nor of his own subjects,<sup id="cite_ref-EBUlrichDuke_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBUlrichDuke-47">[43]</a></sup> who launched the <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Conrad" title="Poor Conrad">Poor Conrad</a></i> rebellion of 1514.<sup id="cite_ref-Peasant_Rebellions_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peasant_Rebellions-48">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tax_Rebellions_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tax_Rebellions-49">[45]</a></sup> Despite this and his rivalry with the Swabian League, his undoing would actually come in the form of his unhappy marriage to <a href="/wiki/Sabina_of_Bavaria" title="Sabina of Bavaria">Sabina of Bavaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBietenholzDeutscher2003464_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBietenholzDeutscher2003464-50">[46]</a></sup> In 1515, Ulrich killed an imperial knight and lover of Sabina's by the name of Hans von Hutten,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup> obliging her to flee to the court of her brother, <a href="/wiki/William_IV,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="William IV, Duke of Bavaria">William IV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Bavaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Bavaria">Duke of Bavaria</a>, who successfully had Ulrich placed under <a href="/wiki/Imperial_ban" title="Imperial ban">Imperial ban</a> twice. When the Emperor died in 1519, Ulrich struck, seizing the Free Imperial City of <a href="/wiki/Reutlingen" title="Reutlingen">Reutlingen</a>, prompting the League to intervene. That same year, Ulrich was soundly defeated and he was and driven into exile in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> following the League's conquest of Württemberg.<sup id="cite_ref-EBUlrichDuke_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBUlrichDuke-47">[43]</a></sup> Württemberg was then sold by the League to Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> who then granted it to his brother, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand I</a>, thus beginning the 12 year ownership of the county by the <a href="/wiki/Habsburgs" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldTravGuide_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldTravGuide-41">[38]</a></sup> When the peasants Ulrich had crushed before rose once again in the <a href="/wiki/German_Peasants%27_War" title="German Peasants' War">German Peasants' War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Peasant_Rebellions_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peasant_Rebellions-48">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tax_Rebellions_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tax_Rebellions-49">[45]</a></sup> Stuttgart was occupied by the peasant armies for a few days in the Spring of 1525. Ulrich, with the help of <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">Philip I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landgrave_of_Hesse" class="mw-redirect" title="Landgrave of Hesse">Landgrave of Hesse</a>, seized the chance to restore himself to power (albeit as an Austrian vassal)<sup id="cite_ref-EBUlrichDuke_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBUlrichDuke-47">[43]</a></sup> in the turmoil of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman-Habsburg_wars#Habsburg_advance" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman-Habsburg wars">War with the</a> <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Turks</a> and invited <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Schnepf" title="Erhard Schnepf">Erhard Schnepf</a> to bring the Reformation to Stuttgart. He accepted, was named Court Preacher in Stuttgart, and worked in concert with <a href="/wiki/Ambrosius_Blarer" title="Ambrosius Blarer">Ambrosius Blarer</a> until his dismissal following his resistance to the <a href="/wiki/Augsburg_Interim" title="Augsburg Interim">Augsburg Interim</a> by the Duke in 1548.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[49]</a></sup> Duke Ulrich himself died two years later, and was succeeded by his son, <a href="/wiki/Christoph,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Christoph, Duke of Württemberg">Christoph</a>. He had grown up in a Württemberg in turmoil, and wished to rebuild its image. To this end, he once again began a construction boom all over the Duchy under the direction of Court Architect <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aberlin_Tretsch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aberlin Tretsch (page does not exist)">Aberlin Tretsch</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuchs200450_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuchs200450-54">[50]</a></sup> knowing full well that the time of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reisek%C3%B6nigtum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Reisekönigtum (page does not exist)">Reisekönigtum</a> was over, Christoph and Tretsch rebuilt and remodeled the Old Castle into a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> palace,<sup id="cite_ref-LanMus:AltSch_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LanMus:AltSch-45">[42]</a></sup> and from 1542–<a href="/w/index.php?title=1544_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1544 in Germany (page does not exist)">44</a>, what is today the <a href="/wiki/Schillerplatz_(Stuttgart)" title="Schillerplatz (Stuttgart)">Schillerplatz</a> was built as a town square.<sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> Duke Christoph also responded to the increasing made for drinking water by embarking upon a massive <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_engineering" title="Hydraulic engineering">hydraulic engineering</a> project in the form of a 2,810 feet (860&#160;m) tunnel to <a href="/w/index.php?title=Park_Lakes_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Park Lakes (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Pffaf Lake</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Glems" title="Glems">Glems</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Nesenbach" title="Nesenbach">Nesenbach</a> from 1566–<a href="/w/index.php?title=1575_in_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="1575 in Germany (page does not exist)">75</a>. In 1575, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Georg_Beer_(architect)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Georg Beer (architect) (page does not exist)">Georg Beer</a> was also appointed Court Architect, and he built the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lusthaus_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lusthaus (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Lusthaus</a>. But it was architect <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heinrich_Schickhardt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Heinrich Schickhardt (page does not exist)">Heinrich Schickhardt</a> who would carry Tretsch's torch further; Schickhardt constructed the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stammheim_Castle_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stammheim Castle (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Stammheim Castle</a> in the suburb of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart-Stammheim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stuttgart-Stammheim (page does not exist)">Stammheim</a>, rebuilt the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fruchtkasten_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fruchtkasten (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Fruchtkasten</a> in the today's Schillerplatz,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> and expanded the Prinzebau.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Thirty Years' War devastated the city,<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt2_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt2-57">[53]</a></sup> and it would slowly decline for a period of time from then on.<sup id="cite_ref-EBStutt_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBStutt-31">[29]</a></sup> After the catastrophic defeat of the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Heilbronn_League" title="Heilbronn League">Heilbronn League</a> by the Habsburgs at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_N%C3%B6rdlingen_(1634)" title="Battle of Nördlingen (1634)">Nörlingen</a> in 1634, <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_III,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Eberhard III, Duke of Württemberg">Duke Eberhard III</a> and his court fled in exile to <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, abandoning the Duchy to looting by pro-Habsburg forces. The Habsburgs once again had full reign of the city for another four years, and in that time Stuttgart had to carry the burden of billeting the pro-Habsburg armies in Swabia. <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand III, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand III</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of the Romans</a>, entered the city in 1634 and, two years later in 1636, once again attempted to re-Catholicize Württemberg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle33_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle33-58">[54]</a></sup> The next year, the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">Bubonic plague</a> struck and devastated the population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle34_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle34-59">[55]</a></sup> The Duke returned in 1638 to a realm somewhat partitioned to Catholic factions in the region, and entirely ravaged by the war. In the Duchy itself, battle, <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> and war reduced the Duchy's population of 350,000 in 1618 to 120,000 in 1648 – about 57% of the population of Württemberg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2009789_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2009789-60">[56]</a></sup> Recovery would be slow for the next several decades, but began nonetheless with the city's first bookstore in 1650 and high school in 1686.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle37_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle37-61">[57]</a></sup> This progress was almost entirely undone when <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">French</a> soldiers under <a href="/wiki/Ez%C3%A9chiel_du_Mas,_Comte_de_M%C3%A9lac" title="Ezéchiel du Mas, Comte de Mélac">Ezéchiel du Mas</a> appeared outside the city's walls in 1688 during the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War" title="Nine Years' War">Nine Years' War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle37_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle37-61">[57]</a></sup> but the city was saved from another sack due to the diplomatic ability of <a href="/wiki/Magdalena_Sibylla_of_Hesse-Darmstadt" title="Magdalena Sibylla of Hesse-Darmstadt">Magdalena Sibylla</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle37_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle37-61">[57]</a></sup> reigning over Württemberg as regent for her son,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_Louis,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Eberhard Louis, Duke of Württemberg">Eberhard Ludwig</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the first time in centuries, Duke Eberhard Ludwig moved the seat of the Duchy out of the declining city of Stuttgart to <a href="/wiki/Ludwigsburg" title="Ludwigsburg">Ludwigsburg</a>, founded in 1704, in 1718 while the namesake <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque</a> <a href="/wiki/Palace" title="Palace">palace</a>, known as the "Versailles of Swabia,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorling2001292_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorling2001292-64">[60]</a></sup> was still under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorling2001292_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorling2001292-64">[60]</a></sup> When Eberhard Ludwig died, his nephew <a href="/wiki/Charles_Alexander,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Charles Alexander, Duke of Württemberg">Charles Alexander</a>, ascended to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt2_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt2-57">[53]</a></sup> Charles Alexander himself died in 1737, meaning his son <a href="/wiki/Charles_Eugene,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Charles Eugene, Duke of Württemberg">Charles Eugene</a> became the premature Duke (and later King) at the age of nine. When he came of age and returned from his tutoring at the court of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_Prussia" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Prussia">King of Prussia</a>, Charles desired to move the capital back to Stuttgart. He commissioned the construction of the <a href="/wiki/New_Palace_(Stuttgart)" title="New Palace (Stuttgart)">New Castle</a> in 1746,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Castle_Solitude" title="Castle Solitude">Castle Solitude</a> in 1763,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Castle_Hohenheim" class="mw-redirect" title="Castle Hohenheim">Castle Hohenheim</a> in 1785,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Karlsschule_Stuttgart" title="Karlsschule Stuttgart">Karlsschule</a> in 1770.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup> The rule of Charles Eugene also saw the tutoring and origins of <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a> in Stuttgart, who studied medicine and completed <a href="/wiki/The_Robbers" title="The Robbers">The Robbers</a> here.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt2_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt2-57">[53]</a></sup> Stuttgart, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/18th_Century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="18th Century in Germany">18th Century</a>, remained a very provincial town of 20,000 residents, narrow alleys, and agriculture and livestock. Despite being the capital and seat of the Duchy, the general staff of the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Army of Württemberg">Army of Württemberg</a> was not present in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDieterle47_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieterle47-69">[65]</a></sup> In 1794, Duke Charles dissolved the Karlsschule to prevent the spreading of revolutionary ideas.</p>
<p>Stuttgart was proclaimed capital once more when Württemberg became an electorate in 1803,<sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> and was yet again named as capital when the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Kingdom of Württemberg">Kingdom of Württemberg</a> was formed in 1805 by the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Pressburg_(1805)" title="Peace of Pressburg (1805)">Peace of Pressburg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Kingdom_of_Württemberg_and_German_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_W.C3.BCrttemberg_and_German_Empire">Kingdom of Württemberg and German Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Württemberg and German Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick of Württemberg">Frederick I</a>'s Württemberg was given high status in the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a> among the College of Kings, and the lands of nearby secondary German states.<sup id="cite_ref-EBConfedRhine_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBConfedRhine-72">[68]</a></sup> Within Stuttgart, the royal residence was expanded under Frederick although many of Stuttgart's most important buildings, including <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Palais" title="Wilhelm Palais">Wilhelm Palace</a>, Katharina Hospital, the <a href="/wiki/Staatsgalerie_Stuttgart" title="Staatsgalerie Stuttgart">State Gallery</a>, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Villa_Berg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Villa Berg (page does not exist)">Villa Berg</a> and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=K%C3%B6nigsbau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Königsbau (page does not exist)">Königsbau</a> were built under the reign of <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="William I of Württemberg">King Wilhelm I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup> In 1818. King Wilhelm I and <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Pavlovna_of_Russia" title="Catherine Pavlovna of Russia">Queen Catherine</a> in an attempt to assuage the suffering caused by the <a href="/wiki/Year_Without_a_Summer" title="Year Without a Summer">Year Without Summer</a> and following famine,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup> introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Cannstatter_Volksfest" title="Cannstatter Volksfest">Cannstatter Volksfest</a> to celebrate the year's bountiful harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-StuttInfo_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StuttInfo-30">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-StadtHist_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StadtHist-33">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hohenheim" title="University of Hohenheim">Hohenheim University</a> was founded in 1818,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup> and two years later the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg_Mausoleum" title="Württemberg Mausoleum">Württemberg Mausoleum</a> as completed on the hill where <a href="/wiki/Wirtemberg_Castle" title="Wirtemberg Castle">Wirtemberg Castle</a> once stood.</p>
<p>From the outset of the <a href="/wiki/19th_Century_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="19th Century in Germany">19th Century</a>, Stuttgart's development was once again impeded by its location (population of the city at the time was around 50,000),<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> but the city began to experience the beginning of economic revival with the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Hauptbahnhof" title="Stuttgart Hauptbahnhof">Main Station</a> in 1846. Prior to then, the signs of rebirth in Stuttgart were evidenced by the construction of such buildings of <a href="/wiki/Rosenstein_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosenstein Castle">Rosenstein Castle</a> in 1822–1830, the <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmspalais" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelmspalais">Wilhelmspalais</a> 1834–1840, and the foundations of the <a href="/wiki/Staatsgalerie_Stuttgart" title="Staatsgalerie Stuttgart">Staatsgalerie</a> in 1843, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Stuttgart" title="University of Stuttgart">University of Stuttgart</a> in 1829,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/State_University_of_Music_and_Performing_Arts_Stuttgart" title="State University of Music and Performing Arts Stuttgart">University of Music and Performing Arts</a> later, in 1857.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup> Stuttgart had a role to play during the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848_in_the_German_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutions of 1848 in the German states">revolution of 1848/1849</a> as well. When internal divisions of the <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Parliament" title="Frankfurt Parliament">Frankfurt Parliament</a> began the demise of that congress, the majority of the Frankfurt Congress voted to move to Stuttgart to flee the reach of the Prussian and Austrian armies in Frankfurt and <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000187_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000187-79">[75]</a></sup> Even though the Congress may have had contacts with revolutionaries in <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Baden" title="Grand Duchy of Baden">Baden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Kingdom of Württemberg">Württemberg</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188-80">[76]</a></sup> the Congress, not popular with the content citizens of Stuttgart,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188-80">[76]</a></sup> were driven out by the King's army.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitchen2000188-80">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stuttgart's literary tradition also bore yet more fruits, being the home of such writers of national importance as <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Hauff" title="Wilhelm Hauff">Wilhelm Hauff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Uhland" title="Ludwig Uhland">Ludwig Uhland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Schwab" title="Gustav Schwab">Gustav Schwab</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eduard_M%C3%B6rike" title="Eduard Mörike">Eduard Mörike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt3_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt3-81">[77]</a></sup> From 1841 to 1846, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jubil%C3%A4umss%C3%A4ule&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jubiläumssäule (page does not exist)">Jubiläumssäule</a> was erected on the <a href="/wiki/Schlossplatz_(Stuttgart)" title="Schlossplatz (Stuttgart)">Schlossplatz</a> before the New Palace according to the plans of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_Michael_Knapp&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Johann Michael Knapp (page does not exist)">Johann Michael Knapp</a> to celebrate the rule of King Wilhelm I.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> A decade later, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=K%C3%B6nigsbau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Königsbau (page does not exist)">Königsbau</a> was constructed by Knapp and court architect <a href="/wiki/Christian_Friedrich_von_Leins" title="Christian Friedrich von Leins">Christian Friedrich von Leins</a> as a concert hall.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup> Another milestone in Stuttgart's history was the running of the first rail line from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bad-Cannstatt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bad-Cannstatt (page does not exist)">Cannstatt</a> to <a href="/wiki/Untert%C3%BCrkheim" class="mw-redirect" title="Untertürkheim">Untertürkheim</a> on 22 October 1845. The advent of <a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">Industrialisation</a> in Germany heralded a major growth of population for Stuttgart: In 1834, Stuttgart counted 35,200 inhabitants,<sup id="cite_ref-PopPDF_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PopPDF-84">[80]</a></sup> rose to 50,000 in 1852, 69,084 inhabitants in 1864,<sup id="cite_ref-PopPDF_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PopPDF-84">[80]</a></sup> and finally 91,000 residents in 1871.<sup id="cite_ref-PopPDF_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PopPDF-84">[80]</a></sup> By 1874, Stuttgart once again exceeded the 100,000 inhabitant mark. This number doubled, due to the incorporation of local towns, to approximately 185,000 in 1901 and then 200,000 in 1904. In 1871, Württemberg joined the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> created by <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minister_President_of_Prussia" title="Minister President of Prussia">Prime Minister of Prussia</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">Unification of Germany</a>, as an autonomous kingdom.</p>
<p>Stuttgart is purported to be the location of the automobile's invention by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Benz" title="Karl Benz">Karl Benz</a> and then industrialized by <a href="/wiki/Gottlieb_Daimler" title="Gottlieb Daimler">Gottlieb Daimler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Maybach" title="Wilhelm Maybach">Wilhelm Maybach</a> in a small workshop in <a href="/wiki/Bad_Cannstatt" title="Bad Cannstatt">Bad Cannstatt</a> that would become <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft" title="Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft">Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft</a> in <a href="/wiki/1887_in_Germany" title="1887 in Germany">1887</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Where_Business_Meets_the_Future_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Where_Business_Meets_the_Future-85">[81]</a></sup> As a result, it is considered to be the starting point of the worldwide automotive industry and is sometimes referred to as the 'cradle of the automobile',<sup id="cite_ref-Amondson_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amondson-22">[20]</a></sup> and today <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a> both have their headquarters in Stuttgart, as well as automotive parts giants <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahle_GmbH" title="Mahle GmbH">Mahle</a>. <a href="/wiki/1886_in_Germany" title="1886 in Germany">The year prior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch" title="Robert Bosch">Robert Bosch</a> opened his first "Workshop for Precision Mechanics and Electrical Engineering" in Stuttgart. In 1907, the <a href="/wiki/International_Socialist_Congress,_Stuttgart_1907" title="International Socialist Congress, Stuttgart 1907">International Socialist Congress</a> was held in Stuttgart was attended by about 60,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1912_in_Germany" title="1912 in Germany">1912</a>, <a href="/wiki/VfB_Stuttgart" title="VfB Stuttgart">VfB Stuttgart</a> was founded.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt3_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt3-81">[77]</a></sup> Two years later, the current iteration of the <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Hauptbahnhof" title="Stuttgart Hauptbahnhof">Stuttgart Hauptbahnhof</a> was completed according to plan by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bonatz" title="Paul Bonatz">Paul Bonatz</a> from <a href="/wiki/1914_in_Germany" title="1914 in Germany">1914</a> to <a href="/wiki/1927_in_Germany" title="1927 in Germany">1927</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the city was accosted by air raids. In 1915, 29 bombs struck the city and the nearby <a href="/w/index.php?title=Roteb%C3%BChlkaserne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rotebühlkaserne (page does not exist)">Rotebühlkaserne</a>, killing four soldiers and injuring another 43, and likewise killing four civilians. The next major air raid on Stuttgart occurred 15 September 1918, when structural damage caused house collapses that killed eleven people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunkel2014132.26ndash.3B135_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunkel2014132.26ndash.3B135-88">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weimar_Republic">Weimar Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Weimar Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the end of the First World War, <a href="/wiki/November_Revolution_of_1918" class="mw-redirect" title="November Revolution of 1918">November revolutionaries</a><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup> stormed the Wilhelmpalais on 30 November 1918 to force King <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Württemberg">Wilhelm II</a> to abdicate, but failed halfway. Under pressure from the revolutionaries, Wilhelm II refused the crown, but also refused to abdicate the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> When he did eventually abdicate, the <a href="/wiki/Freistaat" class="mw-redirect" title="Freistaat">Free State</a> of <a href="/wiki/Free_People%27s_State_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Free People's State of Württemberg">Württemberg</a> was established as a part of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>, and Stuttgart was declared its capital. On 26 April 1919, a new constitution was devised, and the final draft was approved and ratified on 25 September 1919 by the Constituent Assembly. In 1920, Stuttgart temporarily became the seat of the German National Government when the administration fled from <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kapp_Putsch" title="Kapp Putsch">Kapp Putsch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaffner2004225.26ndash.3B226_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaffner2004225.26ndash.3B226-91">[87]</a></sup> Also in 1920, <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Rommel" title="Erwin Rommel">Erwin Rommel</a> became the company commander of the 13th Infantry Regiment based in Stuttgart and would remain as such for the next nine years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler99_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler99-92">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazi_Germany">Nazi Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nazi Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Party</a>'s practice of <a href="/wiki/Gleichschaltung" title="Gleichschaltung">Gleichschaltung</a>, Stuttgart's political importance as state capital became totally nonexistent, though it remained the cultural and economic center of the central <a href="/wiki/Neckar_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Neckar river">Neckar</a> region. Stuttgart, one of the cities bestowed an <a href="/wiki/Honorary_city_titles_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Honorary city titles in Nazi Germany">honorary title</a> by the Nazi regime, was given the moniker "City of the <a href="/wiki/German_diaspora" title="German diaspora">Abroad Germans</a>" in 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-Str.C3.B6lin_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Str.C3.B6lin-93">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stuttg_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stuttg-95">[91]</a></sup> The first prototypes of the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Beetle</a> were manufactured in Stuttgart, according to designs by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Porsche" title="Ferdinand Porsche">Ferdinand Porsche</a>, by a design team including <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Komenda" title="Erwin Komenda">Erwin Komenda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Rabe" title="Karl Rabe">Karl Rabe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hotel_Silber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hotel Silber (page does not exist)">Hotel Silber</a> (English: Silver), previously occupied by other forms of <a href="/wiki/Political_police" class="mw-redirect" title="Political police">political police</a>, was occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a> in 1933 to detain and torture political dissidents.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup> The hotel was used for the transit of Nazi prisoners of conscience including celebrities like <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Bolz" title="Eugen Bolz">Eugen Bolz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Schumacher" title="Kurt Schumacher">Kurt Schumacher</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lilo_Herrmann" class="mw-redirect" title="Lilo Herrmann">Lilo Herrmann</a> to their inevitable deaths in <a href="/wiki/Concentration_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentration camps">concentration camps</a>. The nearby court at Archive Street (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de">Archivstraße</span>) 12A was also used as a central location for executions in Southwest Germany, as the headstone located in its atrium dedicated to the 419 lives lost there recalls.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFederal199587_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFederal199587-99">[95]</a></sup> Participants of the <a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a> burned the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_Synagogue_(Stuttgart)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Old Synagogue (Stuttgart) (page does not exist)">Old Synagogue</a> to the ground<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup> along with the relics contained within and also destroyed its <a href="/wiki/Jewish_cemetery" title="Jewish cemetery">Jewish cemetery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1939_in_Germany" title="1939 in Germany">next year</a> the Nazi regime began the arrests and deportation of Stuttgart's Jewish inhabitants, beginning with the entire male Jewish population of Stuttgart, to the police-run prison camp at <a href="/wiki/Welzheim" title="Welzheim">Welzheim</a> or directly to <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBauzBreugemann2013277_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBauzBreugemann2013277-102">[98]</a></sup> Other Jews from around Württemberg were brought to Stuttgart and housed in the ghetto on the former Trade Fair grounds in <a href="/wiki/Killesbergpark" title="Killesbergpark">Killesberg</a>. As the Memorial at <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_North_station" title="Stuttgart North station">Stuttgart North</a> records,<sup id="cite_ref-northmem_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northmem-103">[99]</a></sup> between 1941 (the first train arrived 1 December 1941, and took around 1,000 men to <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>) and 1945, more than 2,000 Jews from all over Württemberg<sup id="cite_ref-northmem_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northmem-103">[99]</a></sup> were deported to <a href="/wiki/Theresienstadt_concentration_camp" title="Theresienstadt concentration camp">Theresienstadt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a>, and the ghettos at Riga and <a href="/wiki/Izbica_Ghetto" title="Izbica Ghetto">Izbica</a>. Of them, only 180 held in <a href="/wiki/Internment" title="Internment">Internment</a> survived the <a href="/wiki/Shoah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoah">Shoah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFederal199588_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFederal199588-105">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stuttgart, like many of Germany's major cities, was savaged throughout the war by <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Stuttgart_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Stuttgart in World War II">Allied air raids</a>. For the first four years of the war, successful air raids on the city were rare because of the capable defense of the city by <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> ground forces, the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_fog" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial fog">artificial fog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> Despite opinions among some Royal Air Force members that day-time air raids on the city were suicidal,<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> substantial damage to the city's industrial capacity still occurred, such as the 25 August bombing of the Daimler AG plant in 1940 that killed five people.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> With the war increasingly turning against the Third Reich, more and more troops were pulled from the defense of the city in 1943 to fight on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> In 1944, the city center was entirely in ruins due to British and American bombers that could now more easily attack the city. The heaviest raid took place on 12 September 1944, when the Royal Air Force, dropping over 184,000 bombs – including 75 <a href="/wiki/Blockbuster_bomb" title="Blockbuster bomb">blockbusters</a> – leveled Stuttgart's city center, killing 957 people in the resulting <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">firestorm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> In totality, Stuttgart was subjected to 53 bombing raids, resulting in the destruction of 57.7% of all buildings in the city,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[e]</a></sup> the deaths of 4,477 denizens, the disappearance of 85 citizens, and the injury of 8,908 more people.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> The Allies lost 300 aircraft and seven to ten enlisted men.<sup id="cite_ref-GeschStutt4_106-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeschStutt4-106">[102]</a></sup> To commemorate the city citizens who died during the war, the rubble was assembled and used to create the <a href="/wiki/Birkenkopf" title="Birkenkopf">Birkenkopf</a>.</p>
<p>The Allied ground advance into Germany reached Stuttgart in April 1945. Although the attack on the city was to be conducted by the <a href="/wiki/100th_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="100th Infantry Division (United States)">US Seventh Army's 100th Infantry Division</a>, French leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> found this to be unacceptable, as he felt the capture of the region by <a href="/wiki/Free_French_forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French forces">Free French forces</a> would increase French influence in post-war decisions. Independently, he directed <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Lattre_de_Tassigny" title="Jean de Lattre de Tassigny">General de Lattre</a> to order the <a href="/wiki/5th_Armored_Division_(France)" title="5th Armored Division (France)">French 5th Armored Division</a>, <a href="/wiki/2nd_Moroccan_Infantry_Division_(France)" class="mw-redirect" title="2nd Moroccan Infantry Division (France)">2nd Moroccan Infantry Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/3rd_Algerian_Infantry_Division" title="3rd Algerian Infantry Division">3rd Algerian Infantry Division</a> to begin their drive to Stuttgart on 18 April 1945. Two days later, the French forces coordinated with the US Seventh Army and VI Corps heavy artillery, who began a barrage the city. The French 5th Armored Division then captured Stuttgart on 21 April 1945, encountering little resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> The city fared poorly under their direction; French troops forcefully quartered their troops in what housing remained in the city, rapes were frequent (there were at least 1389 recorded incidents of rape of civilians by French soldiers),<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup> and the city's surviving populace were poorly rationed.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[f]</a></sup> The circumstances of what later became known as "The Stuttgart Crisis" provoked political repercussions that reached even the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>. <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> was unable to get De Gaulle to withdraw troops from Stuttgart until after the final boundaries of the zones of occupation were established.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> The French army remained in the city until they finally relented to American demands on 8 July 1945 and withdrew. Stuttgart then became capital of <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg-Baden" title="Württemberg-Baden">Württemberg-Baden</a>, one of the three areas of Allied occupation in Baden-Württemberg, from 1945 until 1952.</p>
<h3><span id="Baden-Württemberg"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Baden-W.C3.BCrttemberg">Baden-Württemberg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Baden-Württemberg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The military government of the <a href="/wiki/American_Occupation_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="American Occupation of Germany">American occupation zone</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Displaced_persons_camp" title="Displaced persons camp">Displaced persons camp</a> for <a href="/wiki/Displaced_person" class="mw-redirect" title="Displaced person">displaced persons</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Forced_labor_under_German_rule_during_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced labor under German rule during World War II">forced labourers</a> from Central and Eastern European industrial firms in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> There was, however, a camp located in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stuttgart-West&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stuttgart-West (page does not exist)">Stuttgart-West</a> that, until its closure and transportation of internees to <a href="/wiki/Heidenheim_an_der_Brenz" title="Heidenheim an der Brenz">Heidenheim an der Brenz</a> in 1949, housed almost exclusively 1400 Jewish survivors of the <a href="/wiki/Shoah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoah">Shoah</a>.</p>
<p>An early concept of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aimed at supporting reconstruction and economic/political recovery across Europe was presented <a href="/wiki/Restatement_of_Policy_on_Germany" title="Restatement of Policy on Germany">during a speech 6 September 1946</a> given by <a href="/wiki/US_Secretary_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="US Secretary of State">US Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/James_F._Byrnes" title="James F. Byrnes">James F. Byrnes</a> at the Stuttgart <a href="/wiki/Staatsoper_Stuttgart" title="Staatsoper Stuttgart">Opera House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> His speech led to the unification of the British and American occupation zones, resulting in the 'bi-zone' (later the 'tri-zone' when the French reluctantly agreed to cede their occupied territory to the new state). In <a href="/wiki/1948_in_Germany" title="1948 in Germany">1948</a>, the city applied to become the capital of the soon to-be <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Republic of Germany">Federal Republic of Germany</a>, and was a serious contender against <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kassel" title="Kassel">Kassel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>. All these cities were examined by the <a href="/wiki/Parlamentarischer_Rat" title="Parlamentarischer Rat">Parlamentarischer Rat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ennen_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ennen-116">[110]</a></sup> but ultimately Bonn won the bid when the Republic was founded on 23 May 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-Ennen_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ennen-116">[110]</a></sup> The city's bid for capital failed primarily because of the financial burdens its high rents would place on the government.</p>
<p>The immediate aftermath of the War would be marked by the controversial efforts of <a href="/wiki/Arnulf_Klett" title="Arnulf Klett">Arnulf Klett</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mayor">Oberbürgermesiter</a> of Stuttgart, to restore the city. Klett favored the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Modernist" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernist">modernist</a> <a href="/wiki/Automotive_city" title="Automotive city">Automotive city</a> with functional divisions for residential, commercial and industrial areas according to the <a href="/wiki/Athens_Charter" title="Athens Charter">Athens Charter</a>. Klett demolished both ruins and entire streets of largely undamaged buildings without rebuilding them to their original visage, a move that earned him much scorn from his contemporaries. In the 150th year since his death (<a href="/wiki/1955_in_Germany" title="1955 in Germany">1955</a>), the last remnant of the alma mater of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Friederich_Schiller&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Friederich Schiller (page does not exist)">Friederich Schiller</a>, the Karlsschule, was removed in favor of an expansion to the <a href="/wiki/Bundesstra%C3%9Fe_14" title="Bundesstraße 14">Bundesstraße 14</a>. Klett also dramatically expanded the <a href="/wiki/Public_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a> of Stuttgart with the <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Stadtbahn" title="Stuttgart Stadtbahn">Stuttgart Stadtbahn</a> and, in <a href="/wiki/1961_in_Germany" title="1961 in Germany">1961</a>, initiated a <a href="/wiki/Twin_towns_and_sister_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Twin towns and sister cities">city partnership</a> with the French city of <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a> as part of an attempt to mend <a href="/wiki/France%E2%80%93Germany_relations" title="France–Germany relations">Franco-German relations</a>.
